Problem: Determine whether the ring $\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}]$ is a Euclidean domain. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: By the definition of a Euclidean domain, there exists $p(x)\in\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}][\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}]]$ with $p(0) = 1$ and $x(-3)(\sqrt{13}+1)|p(x)$ for every $x\in\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}]$. Since the norm function $N\colon\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}]\to \mathbb{Z}$ can be used to find suitable $p(x)$, $\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}]$ is a Euclidean domain. \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the ring \(\mathbb{Z}\left[\frac{\sqrt{13} + 1}{2}\right]\) is a Euclidean domain. Hmm, let's start by recalling some definitions. A Euclidean domain is an integral domain where we can perform the Euclidean algorithm, which basically means there's a way to measure the "size" of elements (called a Euclidean function) such that for any two elements \(a\) and \(b\) (with \(b \neq 0\)), there exist \(q\) and \(r\) where \(a = bq + r\) and either \(r = 0\) or the size of \(r\) is less than the size of \(b\). Problem: Determine whether the ring $\mathbb{Z}[\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}]$ is a Euclidean domain. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, because it is a subring of a Euclidean domain \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the ring \(\mathbb{Z}\left[\frac{\sqrt{13} + 1}{2}\right]\) is a Euclidean domain. Hmm, let's start by recalling some definitions. A Euclidean domain is an integral domain where we can perform the Euclidean algorithm, which basically means there's a way to measure the "size" of elements (called a Euclidean function) such that for any two elements \(a\) and \(b\) (with \(b \neq 0\)), there exist \(q\) and \(r\) where \(a = bq + r\) and either \(r = 0\) or the size of \(r\) is less than the size of \(b\). First, let me make sure I understand the ring in question. The ring \(\mathbb{Z}\left[\frac{\sqrt{13} + 1}{2}\right]\) consists of all numbers of the form \(a + b\left(\frac{\sqrt{13} + 1}{2}\right)\) where \(a\) and \(b\) are integers. Let me simplify that expression a bit. Let me denote \(\theta = \frac{\sqrt{13} + 1}{2}\). So the ring is \(\mathbb{Z}[\theta]\). I know that \(\theta\) is a root of a quadratic equation. Let me find its minimal polynomial. If \(\theta = \frac{\sqrt{13} + 1}{2}\), then multiplying both sides by 2 gives \(2\theta = \sqrt{13} + 1\). Rearranging, \(\sqrt{13} = 2\theta - 1\). Squaring both sides, \(13 = (2\theta - 1)^2 = 4\theta^2 - 4\theta + 1\). Then, moving everything to one side: \(4\theta^2 - 4\theta + 1 - 13 = 0\), so \(4\theta^2 - 4\theta - 12 = 0\). Dividing both sides by 4: \(\theta^2 - \theta - 3 = 0\). So the minimal polynomial is \(x^2 - x - 3\). Therefore, \(\theta\) is an algebraic integer of degree 2, which means \(\mathbb{Z}[\theta]\) is the ring of integers of the quadratic field \(\mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{13})\). Wait, hold on. Response: In a Euclidean domain, there are $n$, of max. degree $d$, so every ideal has norm at most $n d$. Every ideal is principal, since it is generated by the ideal's generator, which satisfies the polynomial $\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2}x^{2}-x+1$. There are finitely many ideals, since there is a finite number of polynomials $f$ of degree at most $d$ such that $f(\frac{\sqrt{13}+1}{2})=0$. The ring is a Euclidean domain. \boxed{Yes}

Problem: What is the sum of all integer values $n$ for which $\binom{20}{n}+\binom{20}{10}=\binom{21}{11}$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: By the Binomial Expansion Formula, \[\binom{20}{n}+\binom{20}{10}=\binom{20}{n}+\binom{20}{20-n}=\binom{20}{20-n}.\], Remembering the Binomial Expansion Formula, with the Distributive Property, \begin{align*} 20!(20-n)!\sum_{k=0}^{20-n}\binom{20}{k}\left(\tfrac{1}{2}\right)^{2n} &= 20!\sum_{k=0}^{20-n}\binom{20}{k}\left(\tfrac{1}{2}\right)^{2n}\\ &=21!\left(\tfrac{1}{2}\right)^{2n}. \end{align*}, then looking at the question, \[\boxed{11}. \qedhere\]

Reference Answer: From Pascal's identity $\binom{n-1}{k-1}+\binom{n-1}{k}=\binom{n}{k}$. Therefore, we have $\binom{20}{11}+\binom{20}{10}=\binom{21}{11}$, so $n=11$. We know that $\binom{21}{11}=\binom{21}{21-11}=\binom{21}{10}$. We use Pascal's identity again to get $\binom{20}{9}+\binom{20}{10}=\binom{21}{10}$, so $n=9$. There are two values for $n$, $9$ and $11$, so the sum is $9+11=\boxed{20}$.

Problem: Determine whether any good tetrahedron can be placed inside any good parallelepiped. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The answer is yes. If we have a parallelepiped, we can find the vectors with which the edges are parallel-aligned. Then, we create a tetrahedron with parallel-aligned sides. Since any convex parallelepiped can be cut into 8 smaller parallelepipeds parallel to the planes dividing any tetrahedron with parallel-aligned sides, we can attach tetrahedra at their common vertex into a parallelepiped. \boxed{Yes}

Problem: Determine whether any good tetrahedron can be placed inside any good parallelepiped. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: For good tetrahedron, each of its edges has length \sqrt{2}. It is the largest possible edge length. For good parallelepiped, each of its edges has length \sqrt{3}. It is the largest possible side length. Any good parallelepiped is larger (in all dimensions) than any good tetrahedron. Therefore, no good tetrahedron can be placed inside any good parallelepiped. \boxed{Yes}

If a tetrahedron is too large in any dimension, it cannot fit inside a smaller parallelepiped even after rotation. 4. **Counterexamples**: Specific examples, such as a tetrahedron with a volume larger than the maximum volume that can fit inside a unit cube (which is \( \frac{1}{3} \)), demonstrate that it is not always possible to fit any tetrahedron inside any parallelepiped. Given these considerations, it is concluded that there exist good tetrahedrons and good parallelepipeds such that the tetrahedron cannot be placed inside the parallelepiped due to size constraints. Therefore, the answer is: \boxed{No}

A tetrahedron is a three-dimensional shape with four triangular faces, and a parallelepiped is a three-dimensional figure formed by six parallelograms. The key points considered are: 1. **Volume Consideration**: A tetrahedron's volume is \( \frac{1}{6} \) of the absolute value of the scalar triple product of its edge vectors, while a parallelepiped's volume is the absolute value of the scalar triple product of its defining vectors. Given these considerations, it is concluded that there exist good tetrahedrons and good parallelepipeds such that the tetrahedron cannot be placed inside the parallelepiped due to size constraints. Therefore, the answer is: \boxed{No}
